<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M87_jet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="1222" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/M87_jet.jpg/200px-M87_jet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/M87_jet.jpg/300px-M87_jet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/M87_jet.jpg/400px-M87_jet.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M87_jet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A jet of particles is being emitted from the core of the elliptical radio galaxy <a href="/wiki/Messier_87" title="Messier 87">M87</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some observable galaxies are classified as "active" if they contain an active galactic nucleus (AGN). A significant portion of the galaxy's total energy output is emitted by the active nucleus instead of its stars, dust and <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>. There are multiple classification and naming schemes for AGNs, but those in the lower ranges of luminosity are called <a href="/wiki/Seyfert_galaxy" title="Seyfert galaxy">Seyfert galaxies</a>, while those with luminosities much greater than that of the host galaxy are known as quasi-stellar objects or <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a>. AGNs emit radiation throughout the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a> from radio wavelengths to X-rays, though some of it may be absorbed by dust or gas associated with the AGN itself or with the host galaxy.
</p><p>The standard model for an <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">active galactic nucleus</a> is based on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">accretion disc</a> that forms around a <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a> (SMBH) at the galaxy's core region. The radiation from an active galactic nucleus results from the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_energy" title="Gravitational energy">gravitational energy</a> of matter as it falls toward the black hole from the disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keel_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-keel-99">[98]</a></sup> The AGN's luminosity depends on the SMBH's mass and the rate at which matter falls onto it.
In about 10% of these galaxies, a diametrically opposed pair of <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_jet" title="Astrophysical jet">energetic jets</a> ejects particles from the galaxy core at velocities close to the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. The mechanism for producing these jets is not well understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-monster_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-monster-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blazars">Blazars</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">Blazar</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">Blazars</a> are believed to be active galaxies with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">relativistic jet</a> pointed in the direction of Earth. A <a href="/wiki/Radio_galaxy" title="Radio galaxy">radio galaxy</a> emits radio frequencies from relativistic jets. A unified model of these types of active galaxies explains their differences based on the observer's position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-monster_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-monster-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="LINERs">LINERs</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Low-ionization_nuclear_emission-line_region" title="Low-ionization nuclear emission-line region">Low-ionization nuclear emission-line region</a></div>
<p>Possibly related to active galactic nuclei (as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starburst_(astronomy)" title="Starburst (astronomy)">starburst</a> regions) are <a href="/wiki/Low-ionization_nuclear_emission-line_region" title="Low-ionization nuclear emission-line region">low-ionization nuclear emission-line regions</a> (LINERs). The emission from LINER-type galaxies is dominated by weakly <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a> elements. The excitation sources for the weakly ionized lines include post-<a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">AGB</a> stars, AGN, and shocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heckman1980_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-heckman1980-101">[100]</a></sup> Approximately one-third of nearby galaxies are classified as containing LINER nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keel_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-keel-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heckman1980_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-heckman1980-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoetal1997b_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-hoetal1997b-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Seyfert_galaxy">Seyfert galaxy</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seyfert_galaxy" title="Seyfert galaxy">Seyfert galaxy</a></div>
<p>Seyfert galaxies are one of the two largest groups of active galaxies, along with quasars. They have quasar-like nuclei (very luminous, distant and bright sources of electromagnetic radiation) with very high surface brightnesses; but unlike quasars, their host galaxies are clearly detectable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson1997_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson1997-103">[102]</a></sup> Seyfert galaxies account for about 10% of all galaxies. Seen in visible light, most look like normal spiral galaxies; but when studied under other wavelengths, their cores' luminosity is equivalent to the luminosity of whole galaxies the size of the Milky Way.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasar">Quasar</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">Quasar</a></div>
<p>Quasars (/ˈkweɪzɑr/) or quasi-stellar radio sources, are the most energetic and distant members of active galactic nuclei. Extremely luminous, they were first identified as high redshift sources of electromagnetic energy, including radio waves and visible light, that appeared more similar to stars than to extended sources similar to galaxies. Their luminosity can be 100 times that of the Milky Way.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Luminous_infrared_galaxy">Luminous infrared galaxy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Luminous_infrared_galaxy" title="Luminous infrared galaxy">Luminous infrared galaxy</a></div>
<p>Luminous infrared galaxies (LIRGs) are galaxies with luminosities—the measurement of electromagnetic power output—above 10<sup>11</sup> L☉ (solar luminosities).  In most cases, most of their energy comes from large numbers of young stars which heat surrounding dust, which reradiates the energy in the infrared.  Luminosity high enough to be a LIRG requires a star formation rate of at least 18 M☉ yr<sup>−1</sup>. Ultra-luminous infrared galaxies (ULIRGs) are at least ten times more luminous still and form stars at rates &gt;180 M☉ yr<sup>−1</sup>.  Many LIRGs also emit radiation from an AGN. Infrared galaxies emit more energy in the infrared than all other wavelengths combined, with peak emission typically at wavelengths of 60 to 100 microns. LIRGs are uncommon in the local universe but were much more common when the universe was younger.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_diameters">Physical diameters</span></h2>
<p>Galaxies do not have a definite boundary by their nature, and are characterized by a gradually decreasing stellar density as a function of increasing distance from their center, making measurements of their true extents difficult. Nevertheless, astronomers over the past few decades have made several criteria  in defining the sizes of galaxies. As early as the time of <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> in 1936, there have been attempts to characterize the diameters of galaxies. With the advent of large sky surveys in the second half of the 20th century, the need for a standard for accurate determination of galaxy sizes has been in greater demand due to its enormous implications in astrophysics, such as the accurate determination of the <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble's law">Hubble constant</a>. Various standards have been adapted over the decades, some more preferred than others. Below are some of these examples.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isophotal_diameter">Isophotal diameter</span></h3>
<p>The <i>isophotal diameter</i> is introduced as a conventional way of measuring a galaxy's size based on its apparent surface brightness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamba_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamba-104">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isophote" title="Isophote">Isophotes</a> are curves in a diagram - such as a picture of a galaxy - that adjoins points of equal brightnesses, and are useful in defining the extent of the galaxy.  The apparent brightness flux of a galaxy is measured in units of magnitudes per square arcsecond (mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup>; sometimes expressed as <i>mag arcsec<sup>-2</sup></i>), which defines the brightness depth of the isophote. To illustrate how this unit works, a typical galaxy has a brightness flux of 18 mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup> at its central region. This brightness is equivalent to the light of an 18th magnitude hypothetical point object (like a star) being spread out evenly in a one square arcsecond area of the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMD_Week_6_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-UMD_Week_6-105">[104]</a></sup> For the purposes of objectivity, the spectrum of light being used is sometimes also given in figures. As an example, the Milky Way has an average surface brightness of 22.1 B-mag/arcsec<sup>-2</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Average_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Average-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodwin_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodwin-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castro-108">[107]</a></sup> where <i>B-mag</i> refers to the brightness at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jhk" title="Jhk">B-band</a> (445 nm wavelength of light, in the blue part of the visible spectrum).
</p><p>R.O. Redman in 1936 suggested that the diameters of galaxies (then referred to as "elliptical nebulae") should be defined at the 25.0 mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup> isophote at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jhk" title="Jhk">B-band</a>, which is expected to cover much of the galaxy's light profile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Redman_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Redman-109">[108]</a></sup> This isophote then became known simply as D<sub>25</sub> (short for "diameter 25"), and corresponds to at least 10% of the normal brightness of the night sky, which is very near the limitations of blue filters at that time. This method was particularly used during the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_General_Catalogue" title="Uppsala General Catalogue">Uppsala General Catalogue</a> using blue filters from the <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Observatory" title="Palomar Observatory">Palomar Observatory</a> Sky Survey in 1972.
</p><p>This conventional standard, however, is not universally agreed upon. Erik Holmberg in 1958 measured the diameters of at least 300 galaxies at the isophote of about 26.5 mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup> (originally defined as where the photographic brightness density with respect to plate background is 0.5%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holmberg1958_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holmberg1958-110">[109]</a></sup> Various other surveys such that of the <a href="/wiki/European_Southern_Observatory" title="European Southern Observatory">ESO</a> in 1989 use isophotes as faint as 27.0 mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESO1989_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESO1989-111">[110]</a></sup> Nevertheless, corrections of these diameters were introduced by both the Second and Third Reference Catalogue of Galaxies (RC2 and RC3), at least to those galaxies being covered by the two catalogues.
</p><p><b>Examples of isophotal diameter measurements:</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a> - 9.86 <a href="/wiki/Parsec#Parsecs_and_kiloparsecs" title="Parsec">kiloparsecs</a> (32,200 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a>) at the 25.0 B-mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup> isophote.</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> - has a diameter at the 25.0 B-mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup> isophote of 26.8 ± 1.1 <a href="/wiki/Parsec#Parsecs_and_kiloparsecs" title="Parsec">kiloparsecs</a> (87,400 ± 3,590 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a>).</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Messier_87" title="Messier 87">Messier 87</a> - has a has a diameter at the 25.0 B-mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup> isophote of  40.55 <a href="/wiki/Parsec#Parsecs_and_kiloparsecs" title="Parsec">kiloparsecs</a> (132,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a>).</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a> - has a has a diameter at the 25.0 B-mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup> isophote of  46.56 <a href="/wiki/Parsec#Parsecs_and_kiloparsecs" title="Parsec">kiloparsecs</a> (152,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a>).</li></ul>
<h3><span id="Effective_radius_.28half-light.29_and_its_variations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Effective_radius_(half-light)_and_its_variations">Effective radius (half-light) and its variations</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-light_radius" title="Half-light radius">half-light radius</a> (also known as <i>effective radius</i>; R<sub><i>e</i></sub>) is a measure that is based on the galaxy's overall brightness flux. This is the radius upon which half, or 50%, of the total brightness flux of the galaxy was emitted. This was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_de_Vaucouleurs" title="Gérard de Vaucouleurs">Gérard de Vaucouleurs</a> in 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaucouleurs_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaucouleurs-112">[111]</a></sup> The choice of using 50% was arbitrary, but proved to be useful in further works by R. A. Fish in 1963,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fish_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fish-113">[112]</a></sup> where he established a luminosity concentration law that relates the brightnesses of elliptical galaxies and their respective R<sub><i>e</i></sub>, and by J.L. Sérsic in 1968<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sersic_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sersic-114">[113]</a></sup> that defined a mass-radius relation in galaxies.
</p><p>In defining R<sub><i>e</i></sub>, it is necessary that the overall brightness flux galaxy should be captured, with a method employed by Bershady in 2000 suggesting to measure twice the size where the brightness flux of an arbitrarily chosen radius, defined as the local flux, divided by the overall average flux equals to 0.2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bershady_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bershady-115">[114]</a></sup> Using half-light radius allows a rough estimate of a galaxy's size, but is not particularly helpful in determining its morphology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpiralDisks_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpiralDisks-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Variations of this method exist. In particular, in the ESO-Uppsala Catalogue of Galaxies values of 50%, 70%, and 90% of the total blue light (the light detected through a B-band specific filter) had been used to calculate a galaxy's diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESO_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESO-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petrosian_magnitude">Petrosian magnitude</span></h3>
<p>First described by V. Petrosian in 1976,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petrosian_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petrosian-118">[117]</a></sup> a modified version of this method has been utilized by the <a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">Sloan Digital Sky Survey</a> (SDSS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdss_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdss-119">[118]</a></sup> This method employs a mathematical model on a galaxy whose radius is determined by the azimuthally (horizontal) averaged profile of its brightness flux. In particular, the SDSS employed the Petrosian magnitude in the R-band (658 nm, in the red part of the visible spectrum) to ensure that the brightness flux of a galaxy would be captured as much as possible while counteracting the effects of background noise. For a galaxy whose brightness profile is exponential, it is expected to capture all of its brightness flux, and 80% for galaxies that follow a profile under <a href="/wiki/De_Vaucouleurs%27s_law" title="De Vaucouleurs's law">de Vaucouleurs's law</a>.
</p><p>Petrosian magnitudes have the advantage that it is redshift and distance independent, allowing the measurement of the galaxy's apparent size since the Petrosian radius is defined in terms of the galaxy's overall luminous flux.
</p><p>A critique of an earlier version of this method has been issued by IPAC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipac1_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipac1-120">[119]</a></sup> with the method causing a magnitude of error (upwards to 10%) of the values than using isophotal diameter. The use of Petrosian magnitudes also have the disadvantage of missing most of the light outside the Petrosian aperture, which is defined relative to the galaxy's overall brightness profile, especially for elliptical galaxies, with higher signal-to-noise ratios on higher distances and redshifts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ned2_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ned2-121">[120]</a></sup> A correction for this method has been issued by Graham <i>et al</i> in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham-122">[121]</a></sup> based on the assumption that galaxies follow Sersic's law.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Near-infrared_method">Near-infrared method</span></h3>
<p>This method has been used by <a href="/wiki/2MASS" title="2MASS">2MASS</a> as an adaptation from the previously used methods of isophotal measurement. Since 2MASS operates in the near infrared, which has the advantage of being able to recognize dimmer, cooler, and older stars, it has a different form of approach compared to other methods that normally use B-filter. The detail of the method used by 2MASS has been described thoroughly in a document by Jarrett <i>et al</i>, with the survey measuring several parameters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarrett_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jarrett-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The standard aperture ellipse (area of detection) is defined by the infrared isophote at the <a href="/wiki/K_band_(infrared)" title="K band (infrared)">K<sub>s</sub> band</a> (roughly 2.2 μm wavelength) of 20 mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup>. Gathering the overall luminous flux of the galaxy has been employed by at least four methods: the first being a circular aperture extending 7 arcseconds from the center, an isophote at 20 mag/arcsec<sup>2</sup>, a "total" aperture defined by the radial light distribution that covers the supposed extent of the galaxy, and the Kron aperture (defined as 2.5 times the first-moment radius, an integration of the flux of the "total" aperture).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarrett_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jarrett-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_fields">Magnetic fields</span></h3>
<p>Galaxies have <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galactic_magnetic_fields_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-galactic_magnetic_fields-124">[123]</a></sup> They are strong enough to be dynamically important, as they:
</p>
<ul><li>Drive mass inflow into the centers of galaxies</li>
<li>Modify the formation of spiral arms</li>
<li>Can affect the rotation of gas in the galaxies' outer regions</li>
<li>Provide the transport of angular momentum required for the collapse of gas clouds, and hence the formation of new stars</li></ul>
<p>The typical average <a href="/wiki/Equipartition_theorem" title="Equipartition theorem">equipartition</a> strength for <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral galaxies</a> is about 10 μG (<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">microgauss</a>) or 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>nT (<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">nanotesla</a>). By comparison, the Earth's magnetic field has an average strength of about 0.3 G (Gauss or 30 μT (<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">microtesla</a>). Radio-faint galaxies like <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">M 31</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triangulum_Galaxy" title="Triangulum Galaxy">M33</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>'s neighbors, have weaker fields (about 5<span class="nowrap"> </span>μG), while gas-rich galaxies with high star-formation rates, like M 51, M 83 and NGC 6946, have 15 μG on average. In prominent spiral arms, the field strength can be up to 25 μG, in regions where cold gas and dust are also concentrated. The strongest total equipartition fields (50–100 μG) were found in <a href="/wiki/Starburst_galaxy" title="Starburst galaxy">starburst galaxies</a>—for example, in M 82 and the <a href="/wiki/Antennae_Galaxies" title="Antennae Galaxies">Antennae</a>; and in nuclear starburst regions, such as the centers of NGC 1097 and other <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred galaxies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galactic_magnetic_fields_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-galactic_magnetic_fields-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">Galaxy formation and evolution</a></div>
<p>Galactic formation and evolution is an active area of research in <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2550" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of a protocluster forming in the early universe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Current models of the formation of galaxies in the early universe are based on the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">ΛCDM</a> model. About 300,000 years after the big bang, atoms of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> began to form, in an event called <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination</a>. Nearly all the hydrogen was neutral (non-ionized) and readily absorbed light, and no stars had yet formed. As a result, this period has been called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang#Dark_Ages" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">dark ages</a>". It was from density fluctuations (or <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropic</a> irregularities) in this primordial matter that <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">larger structures</a> began to appear. As a result, masses of <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> matter started to condense within <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> halos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hqrdvj_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-hqrdvj-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rmaa17_107_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-rmaa17_107-127">[126]</a></sup> These primordial structures eventually became the galaxies we see today.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg/220px-Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg/330px-Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg/440px-Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of a young galaxy accreting material</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_galaxy_formation">Early galaxy formation</span></h4>
<p>Evidence for the appearance of galaxies very early in the Universe's history was found in 2006, when it was discovered that the galaxy <a href="/wiki/IOK-1" title="IOK-1">IOK-1</a> has an unusually high <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of 6.96, corresponding to just 750 million years after the Big Bang and making it the most distant and earliest-to-form galaxy seen at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup>
While some scientists have claimed other objects (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_Abell_1835_IR1916" title="Galaxy Abell 1835 IR1916">Abell 1835 IR1916</a>) have higher redshifts (and therefore are seen in an earlier stage of the universe's evolution), IOK-1's age and composition have been more reliably established. In December 2012, astronomers reported that <a href="/wiki/UDFj-39546284" title="UDFj-39546284">UDFj-39546284</a> is the most distant object known and has a redshift value of 11.9. The object, estimated to have existed around 380 million years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20121212_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20121212-129">[128]</a></sup> after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> (which was about 13.8 billion years ago),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cosmic_Detectives_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmic_Detectives-130">[129]</a></sup> is about 13.42 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distance_measures_(cosmology)" title="Distance measures (cosmology)">light travel distance years</a> away. The existence of galaxies so soon after the Big Bang suggests that <a href="/wiki/Protogalaxy" title="Protogalaxy">protogalaxies</a> must have grown in the so-called "dark ages".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hqrdvj_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-hqrdvj-126">[125]</a></sup> As of May 5, 2015, the galaxy <a href="/wiki/EGS-zs8-1" title="EGS-zs8-1">EGS-zs8-1</a> is the most distant and earliest galaxy measured, forming 670 million years after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. The light from EGS-zs8-1 has taken 13 billion years to reach Earth, and is now 30 billion light-years away, because of the <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">expansion of the universe</a> during 13 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys.org_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-phys.org-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> On 17 August 2022, NASA released a large mosaic image of 690 individual frames taken by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_Infrared_Camera" title="Near Infrared Camera">Near Infrared Camera</a> (NIRCam) on the <a href="/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope" title="James Webb Space Telescope">James Webb Space Telescope</a> (JWST) of numerous very early galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UT-20220817_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-UT-20220817-136">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ES-20220818_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-ES-20220818-137">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20220823_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20220823-138">[137]</a></sup> Some early galaxies observed by JWST, like <a href="/wiki/CEERS-93316" title="CEERS-93316">CEERS-93316</a>, a candidate high-redshift galaxy, has an estimated <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of approximately z = 16.7, corresponding to 235.8 million years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDIN-20220801_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-EDIN-20220801-139">[138]</a></sup> after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck_2018age_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2018age-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="929" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg/220px-Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg/330px-Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg/440px-Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different components of near-infrared background light detected by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in deep-sky surveys<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The detailed process by which the earliest galaxies formed is an open question in astrophysics. Theories can be divided into two categories: top-down and bottom-up. In top-down correlations (such as the Eggen–Lynden-Bell–Sandage [ELS] model), protogalaxies form in a large-scale simultaneous collapse lasting about one hundred million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> In bottom-up theories (such as the Searle-Zinn [SZ] model), small structures such as <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a> form first, and then a number of such bodies accrete to form a larger galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup>
Once protogalaxies began to form and contract, the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halo_star" title="Halo star">halo stars</a> (called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_3_stars" title="Population 3 stars">Population III stars</a>) appeared within them. These were composed almost entirely of hydrogen and helium and may have been more massive than 100 times the Sun's mass. If so, these huge stars would have quickly consumed their supply of fuel and became <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>, releasing heavy elements into the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> This first generation of stars re-ionized the surrounding neutral hydrogen, creating expanding bubbles of space through which light could readily travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2015, astronomers reported evidence for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_3_stars" title="Population 3 stars">Population III stars</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos_Redshift_7" title="Cosmos Redshift 7">Cosmos Redshift 7</a> galaxy at <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 6.60</span>. Such stars are likely to have existed in the very early universe (i.e., at high redshift), and may have started the production of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> heavier than <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> that are needed for the later formation of planets and life as we know it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-20150604_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-20150604-146">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150617_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150617-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<p>Within a billion years of a galaxy's formation, key structures begin to appear. <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">Globular clusters</a>, the central supermassive black hole, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">galactic bulge</a> of metal-poor <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">Population II stars</a> form. The creation of a supermassive black hole appears to play a key role in actively regulating the growth of galaxies by limiting the total amount of additional matter added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> During this early epoch, galaxies undergo a major burst of star formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the following two billion years, the accumulated matter settles into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disc_(galaxy)" title="Disc (galaxy)">galactic disc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> A galaxy will continue to absorb infalling material from <a href="/wiki/High-velocity_cloud" title="High-velocity cloud">high-velocity clouds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarf galaxies</a> throughout its life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> This matter is mostly hydrogen and helium. The cycle of stellar birth and death slowly increases the abundance of heavy elements, eventually allowing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_formation" title="Planetary formation">formation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">Hubble eXtreme Deep Field (XDF)</a></i></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XDF-scale.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2489" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/XDF-scale.jpg/200px-XDF-scale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/XDF-scale.jpg/300px-XDF-scale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/XDF-scale.jpg/400px-XDF-scale.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">XDF</a></i> view field compared to the <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular size</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Several thousand galaxies, each consisting of billions of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, are in this small view.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2078" data-file-width="2382" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/200px-The_Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/300px-The_Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/The_Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/400px-The_Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">XDF</a></i> (2012) view: Each light speck is a galaxy, some of which are as old as 13.2 billion years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120925_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120925-153">[152]</a></sup> – the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> is estimated to contain 200 billion to two trillion galaxies.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XDF-separated.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2101" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/200px-XDF-separated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/300px-XDF-separated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/400px-XDF-separated.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">XDF</a></i> image shows (from left) fully mature galaxies, nearly mature galaxies (from five to nine billion years ago), and <a href="/wiki/Protogalaxy" title="Protogalaxy">protogalaxies</a>, blazing with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_star" title="Young star">young stars</a> (beyond nine billion years).</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The evolution of galaxies can be significantly affected by interactions and collisions. Mergers of galaxies were common during the early epoch, and the majority of galaxies were peculiar in morphology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa296_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa296-154">[153]</a></sup> Given the distances between the stars, the great majority of stellar systems in colliding galaxies will be unaffected. However, gravitational stripping of the interstellar gas and dust that makes up the spiral arms produces a long train of stars known as tidal tails. Examples of these formations can be seen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGC_4676" title="NGC 4676">NGC 4676</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Antennae_Galaxies" title="Antennae Galaxies">Antennae Galaxies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Milky Way galaxy and the nearby Andromeda Galaxy are moving toward each other at about 130 <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">km/s</a>, and—depending upon the lateral movements—the two might collide in about five to six billion years. Although the Milky Way has never collided with a galaxy as large as Andromeda before, evidence of past collisions of the Milky Way with smaller dwarf galaxies is increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Such large-scale interactions are rare. As time passes, mergers of two systems of equal size become less common. Most bright galaxies have remained fundamentally unchanged for the last few billion years, and the net rate of star formation probably also peaked about ten billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_trends">Future trends</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Future_of_an_expanding_universe" title="Future of an expanding universe">Future of an expanding universe</a></div>
<p>Spiral galaxies, like the Milky Way, produce new generations of stars as long as they have dense <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a> of interstellar hydrogen in their spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> Elliptical galaxies are largely devoid of this gas, and so form few new stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> The supply of star-forming material is finite; once stars have converted the available supply of hydrogen into heavier elements, new star formation will come to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmic_battle_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosmic_battle-161">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current era of star formation is expected to continue for up to one hundred billion years, and then the "stellar age" will wind down after about ten trillion to one hundred trillion years (10<sup>13</sup>–10<sup>14</sup> years), as the smallest, longest-lived stars in the visible universe, tiny <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a>, begin to fade. At the end of the stellar age, galaxies will be composed of <a href="/wiki/Compact_star" title="Compact star">compact objects</a>: <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a> that are cooling or cold ("<a href="/wiki/Black_dwarf" title="Black dwarf">black dwarfs</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>. Eventually, as a result of <a href="/wiki/Relaxation_(physics)#Relaxation_in_astronomy" title="Relaxation (physics)">gravitational relaxation</a>, all stars will either fall into central supermassive black holes or be flung into intergalactic space as a result of collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmic_battle_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-cosmic_battle-161">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Larger-scale_structures">Larger-scale structures</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#Large-scale_structure" title="Observable universe">Observable universe § Large-scale structure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">Galaxy filament</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">Galaxy groups and clusters</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1392" data-file-width="1488" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg/230px-Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg/345px-Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg/460px-Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Seyfert%27s_Sextet" title="Seyfert's Sextet">Seyfert's Sextet</a> is an example of a compact galaxy group.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Deep-sky surveys show that galaxies are often found in groups and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clusters_of_galaxies" title="Clusters of galaxies">clusters</a>. Solitary galaxies that have not significantly interacted with other galaxies of comparable mass in the past billion years are relatively scarce. Only about 5% of the galaxies surveyed are truly isolated; however, they may have interacted and even merged with other galaxies in the past, and may still be orbited by smaller satellite galaxies. Isolated galaxies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[note 2]</a></sup> can produce stars at a higher rate than normal, as their gas is not being stripped by other nearby galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the largest scale, the universe is continually expanding, resulting in an average increase in the separation between individual galaxies (see <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble's law">Hubble's law</a>). Associations of galaxies can overcome this expansion on a local scale through their mutual gravitational attraction. These associations formed early, as clumps of dark matter pulled their respective galaxies together. Nearby groups later merged to form larger-scale clusters. This ongoing merging process (as well as an influx of infalling gas) heats the intergalactic gas in a cluster to very high temperatures of 30–100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megakelvin" title="Megakelvin">megakelvins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup> About 70–80% of a cluster's mass is in the form of dark matter, with 10–30% consisting of this heated gas and the remaining few percent in the form of galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most galaxies are gravitationally bound to a number of other galaxies. These form a <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a>-like hierarchical distribution of clustered structures, with the smallest such associations being termed groups. A group of galaxies is the most common type of galactic cluster; these formations contain the majority of galaxies (as well as most of the <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> mass) in the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup> To remain gravitationally bound to such a group, each member galaxy must have a sufficiently low velocity to prevent it from escaping (see <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">Virial theorem</a>). If there is insufficient <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, however, the group may evolve into a smaller number of galaxies through mergers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p><br/>
Clusters of galaxies consist of hundreds to thousands of galaxies bound together by gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_protocluster_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubble_protocluster-171">[169]</a></sup> Clusters of galaxies are often dominated by a single giant elliptical galaxy, known as the <a href="/wiki/Brightest_cluster_galaxy" title="Brightest cluster galaxy">brightest cluster galaxy</a>, which, over time, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidally</a> destroys its satellite galaxies and adds their mass to its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1053" data-file-width="4746" decoding="async" height="49" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg/220px-The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg/330px-The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg/440px-The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Southern plane of the Milky Way from submillimeter wavelengths<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">Superclusters</a> contain tens of thousands of galaxies, which are found in clusters, groups and sometimes individually. At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_Cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the Cosmos">supercluster scale</a>, galaxies are arranged into sheets and filaments surrounding vast empty voids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup> Above this scale, the universe appears to be the same in all directions (<a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropic</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Homogeneity" title="wikt:Homogeneity">homogeneous</a>).,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup> though this notion has been challenged in recent years by numerous findings of large-scale structures that appear to be exceeding this scale. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hercules-Corona_Borealis_Great_Wall" title="Hercules-Corona Borealis Great Wall">Hercules-Corona Borealis Great Wall</a>, currently the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_cosmic_structures" title="List of largest cosmic structures">largest structure</a> in the universe found so far, is 10 billion <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (three gigaparsecs) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBHT2_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBHT2-176">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HBHT_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-HBHT-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cookie_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-cookie-178">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Milky Way galaxy is a member of an association named the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a>, a relatively small group of galaxies that has a diameter of approximately one megaparsec. The Milky Way and the Andromeda Galaxy are the two brightest galaxies within the group; many of the other member galaxies are dwarf companions of these two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup> The Local Group itself is a part of a cloud-like structure within the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster" title="Virgo Supercluster">Virgo Supercluster</a>, a large, extended structure of groups and clusters of galaxies centered on the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Cluster" title="Virgo Cluster">Virgo Cluster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tully1982_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-tully1982-180">[178]</a></sup> And the Virgo Supercluster itself is a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pisces-Cetus_Supercluster_Complex" title="Pisces-Cetus Supercluster Complex">Pisces-Cetus Supercluster Complex</a>, a giant <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">galaxy filament</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-wavelength_observation">Multi-wavelength observation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">Observational astronomy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andromeda_galaxy.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="6200" data-file-width="6200" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/220px-Andromeda_galaxy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/330px-Andromeda_galaxy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/440px-Andromeda_galaxy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">This ultraviolet image of <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda</a> shows blue regions containing young, massive stars.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The peak radiation of most stars lies in the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>, so the observation of the stars that form galaxies has been a major component of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_astronomy" title="Optical astronomy">optical astronomy</a>. It is also a favorable portion of the spectrum for observing ionized <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">H II regions</a>, and for examining the distribution of dusty arms.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> present in the interstellar medium is opaque to visual light. It is more transparent to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_infrared_astronomy" title="Far infrared astronomy">far-infrared</a>, which can be used to observe the interior regions of giant molecular clouds and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">galactic cores</a> in great detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> Infrared is also used to observe distant, <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">red-shifted</a> galaxies that were formed much earlier. Water vapor and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> absorb a number of useful portions of the infrared spectrum, so high-altitude or space-based telescopes are used for <a href="/wiki/Infrared_astronomy" title="Infrared astronomy">infrared astronomy</a>.
</p><p>The first non-visual study of galaxies, particularly active galaxies, was made using <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio frequencies</a>. The Earth's atmosphere is nearly transparent to radio between 5 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">MHz</a> and 30 GHz. (The <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> blocks signals below this range.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup> Large radio <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">interferometers</a> have been used to map the active jets emitted from active nuclei. <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">Radio telescopes</a> can also be used to observe neutral hydrogen (via <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">21 cm radiation</a>), including, potentially, the non-ionized matter in the early universe that later collapsed to form galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_astronomy" title="UV astronomy">Ultraviolet</a> and <a href="/wiki/X-ray_astronomy" title="X-ray astronomy">X-ray telescopes</a> can observe highly energetic galactic phenomena. Ultraviolet flares are sometimes observed when a star in a distant galaxy is torn apart from the tidal forces of a nearby black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup> The distribution of hot gas in galactic clusters can be mapped by X-rays. The existence of supermassive black holes at the cores of galaxies was confirmed through X-ray astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Galaxies to the left side of the Hubble classification scheme are sometimes referred to as "early-type", while those to the right are "late-type".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term "field galaxy" is sometimes used to mean an isolated galaxy, although the same term is also used to describe galaxies that do not belong to a cluster but may be a member of a group of galaxies.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Wright, <i>An Original Theory or New Hypothesis of the Universe</i><span class="nowrap"> </span>... (London, England: H. Chapelle, 1750). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA48" rel="nofollow">From p.48:</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20161120194825/https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA48" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> November 20, 2016, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> "...<span class="nowrap"> </span>the stars are not infinitely dispersed and distributed in a promiscuous manner throughout all the mundane space, without order or design,<span class="nowrap"> </span>... this phænomenon [is] no other than a certain effect arising from the observer's situation,<span class="nowrap"> </span>... To a spectator placed in an indefinite space,<span class="nowrap"> </span>... it [i.e., the Milky Way (<i>Via Lactea</i>)] [is] a vast ring of stars<span class="nowrap"> </span>..."<br/>
<a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA73" rel="nofollow">On page 73</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20161120194830/https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA73" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> November 20, 2016, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, Wright called the Milky Way the <i>Vortex Magnus</i> (the great whirlpool) and estimated its diameter at 8.64×10<sup>12</sup> miles (13.9×10<sup>12</sup> km).</span>
</li>
